<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Edward II of England</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Edward II</b> (25 April 1284 – 21 September 1327), also called <b>Edward of Caernarfon</b>, was <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">King of England</a> from 1307 until he was <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(politics)" title="Deposition (politics)">deposed</a> in January 1327. The fourth son of <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a>, Edward became the heir to the throne following the death of his older brother <a href="/wiki/Alphonso,_Earl_of_Chester" title="Alphonso, Earl of Chester">Alphonso</a>. Beginning in 1300, Edward accompanied his father on <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">campaigns to pacify Scotland</a>, and in 1306 he was <a href="/wiki/Knight#Origins_of_medieval_knighthood" title="Knight">knighted</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Swans" title="Feast of the Swans">grand ceremony</a> at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. Edward succeeded to the throne in 1307, following his father's death. In 1308, he married <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella of France</a>, the daughter of the powerful <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">King Philip IV</a>, as part of a long-running effort to resolve the tensions between the English and French crowns.</p>
<p>Edward had a close and controversial relationship with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piers_Gaveston" title="Piers Gaveston">Piers Gaveston</a>, who had joined his household in 1300. The precise nature of Edward and Gaveston's relationship is uncertain; they may have been friends, lovers or <a href="/wiki/Blood_brother" title="Blood brother">sworn brothers</a>. Gaveston's arrogance and power as Edward's <a href="/wiki/Favourite" title="Favourite">favourite</a> provoked discontent both among the <a href="/wiki/Baron#United_Kingdom_and_the_Commonwealth" title="Baron">barons</a> and the French royal family, and Edward was forced to <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">exile</a> him. On Gaveston's return, the barons pressured the King into agreeing to wide-ranging reforms called the <a href="/wiki/Ordinances_of_1311" title="Ordinances of 1311">Ordinances of 1311</a>. The newly empowered barons banished Gaveston, to which Edward responded by revoking the reforms and recalling his favourite. Led by Edward's cousin, the <a href="/wiki/Thomas,_2nd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Thomas, 2nd Earl of Lancaster">Earl of Lancaster</a>, a group of the barons seized and executed Gaveston in 1312, beginning several years of armed confrontation. English forces were pushed back in Scotland, where Edward was decisively defeated by <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314. <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%9317" title="Great Famine of 1315–17">Widespread famine</a> followed, and criticism of the King's reign mounted.</p>
<p>The Despenser family, in particular <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Despenser_the_Younger" title="Hugh Despenser the Younger">Hugh Despenser the Younger</a>, became close friends and advisers to Edward, but in 1321 Lancaster and many of the barons seized the Despensers' lands and forced the King to exile them. In response, Edward led a <a href="/wiki/Despenser_War" title="Despenser War">short military campaign</a>, capturing and executing Lancaster. Edward and the Despensers strengthened their grip on power, revoking the 1311 reforms, executing their enemies and confiscating estates. Unable to make progress in Scotland, Edward finally signed a truce with Robert. Opposition to the regime grew, and when Isabella was sent to France to negotiate a <a href="/wiki/War_of_Saint-Sardos#Aftermath" title="War of Saint-Sardos">peace treaty</a> in 1325, she turned against Edward and refused to return. Isabella allied herself with the exiled <a href="/wiki/Roger_Mortimer,_1st_Earl_of_March" title="Roger Mortimer, 1st Earl of March">Roger Mortimer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_England_(1326)" title="Invasion of England (1326)">invaded England</a> with a small army in 1326. Edward's regime collapsed and he fled into Wales, where he was captured in November. Edward was forced to relinquish his crown in January 1327 in favour of his fourteen-year-old son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, and he died in <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Castle" title="Berkeley Castle">Berkeley Castle</a> on 21 September, probably murdered on the orders of the new regime.</p>
<p>Edward's relationship with Gaveston inspired <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>'s 1592 play <i><a href="/wiki/Edward_II_(play)" title="Edward II (play)">Edward II</a></i>, along with other plays, films, novels and media. Many of these have focused on the possible sexual relationship between the two men. Edward's contemporaries criticised his performance as a king, noting his failures in Scotland and the oppressive regime of his later years, although 19th-century academics later argued that the growth of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">parliamentary institutions</a> during his reign was a positive development for England over the longer term. Debate has continued into the 21st century as to whether Edward was a lazy and incompetent king, or simply a reluctant and ultimately unsuccessful ruler.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Issue"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Issue</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Ancestry"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestry</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Edward II was the fourth son of <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a> and his first wife, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Castile" title="Eleanor of Castile">Eleanor of Castile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> His father was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_England" title="King of England">King of England</a>, and had also inherited <a href="/wiki/Gascony" title="Gascony">Gascony</a> in south-western France, which he held as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal" title="Feudal">feudal</a> vassal of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_France" title="King of France">King of France</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> His mother was from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castilian</a> royal family, and held the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County_of_Ponthieu" title="County of Ponthieu">County of Ponthieu</a> in northern France. Edward I proved to be a successful military leader, leading the <a href="/wiki/Second_Barons%27_War" title="Second Barons' War">suppression of the baronial revolts</a> in the 1260s, and joining the <a href="/wiki/Ninth_Crusade" title="Ninth Crusade">Ninth Crusade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> During the 1280s he <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Wales_by_Edward_I" title="Conquest of Wales by Edward I">conquered North Wales</a>, removing the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">native Welsh</a> princes from power, and in the 1290s he <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_independence" title="Wars of Scottish independence">intervened in Scotland's civil war</a>, claiming <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> over the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> He was considered an extremely successful ruler by his contemporaries, largely able to control the powerful <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earls</a> that formed the senior ranks of the English nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Michael_Prestwich" title="Michael Prestwich">Michael Prestwich</a> describes Edward I as "a king to inspire fear and respect", while <a href="/wiki/John_Gillingham" title="John Gillingham">John Gillingham</a> characterises him as an efficient bully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite his successes, when Edward I died in 1307 he left a range of challenges for his son to resolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> One of the most critical was the problem of English rule in Scotland, where Edward's long but ultimately inconclusive military campaign was ongoing when he died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Edward's control of Gascony created tension with the French kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown1988P575_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1988P575-9">[9]</a></sup> They insisted that the English kings should give <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage</a> to them for the lands; the English kings saw this demand as insulting to their honour, and the issue remained unresolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown1988P575_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1988P575-9">[9]</a></sup> Edward I also faced increasing opposition from his barons over the taxation and requisitions required to resource his wars, and left his son debts of around £200,000 on his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[nb 1]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_.281284.E2.80.931307.29">Early life (1284–1307)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life (1284–1307)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Birth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of Caernarfon castle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg/220px-Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg/330px-Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg/440px-Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caernarfon_Castle_1994.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Caernarfon_Castle" title="Caernarfon Castle">Caernarfon Castle</a>, Edward's birthplace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward II was born in <a href="/wiki/Caernarfon_Castle" title="Caernarfon Castle">Caernarfon Castle</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a> on 25 April 1284, less than a year after Edward I had conquered the region, and as a result is sometimes called Edward of Caernarfon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> The King probably deliberately chose the castle as the location for Edward's birth, as it was an important symbolic location for the native Welsh, associated with <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman imperial history</a>, and it formed the centre of the new royal administration of North Wales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Edward's birth brought with it predictions of greatness from contemporary <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophets</a> who believed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_Days" title="Last Days">Last Days</a> of the world were imminent, declaring him to be a new <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a>, who would lead England to glory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Powel" title="David Powel">David Powel</a>, a 16th-century clergyman, suggested that the baby was offered to the Welsh as a prince "that was borne in Wales and could speake never a word of English", but there is no evidence to support this account.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward's name was <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">English</a> in origin, linking him to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, and was chosen by his father instead of the more traditional <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castilian_language" title="Castilian language">Castilian</a> names selected for Edward's brothers:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> Edward had three elder brothers: John and Henry who had died before Edward was born, and <a href="/wiki/Alphonso,_Earl_of_Chester" title="Alphonso, Earl of Chester">Alphonso</a>, who died in August 1284, leaving Edward as the heir to the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P40_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P40-18">[17]</a></sup> Although Edward was a relatively healthy child, there were enduring concerns throughout his early years that he too might die and leave his father without a male heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P40_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P40-18">[17]</a></sup> After his birth, Edward was looked after by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wet-nurse" title="Wet-nurse">wet-nurse</a> called Mariota or Mary Maunsel for a few months until she fell ill, when Alice de Leygrave became his foster mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhillipsChaplaisHaines_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhillipsChaplaisHaines-19">[18]</a></sup> He would have barely known his natural mother Eleanor, who was in Gascony with his father during his earliest years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhillipsChaplaisHaines_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-PhillipsChaplaisHaines-19">[18]</a></sup> An official household complete with staff was created for the new baby, under the direction of a clerk, Giles of Oudenarde.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood.2C_personality_and_appearance">Childhood, personality and appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Childhood, personality and appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Edward" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="366" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg/170px-Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg/255px-Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg/340px-Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gal_nations_edward_i.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>, thought to be of Edward's father, <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Spending on Edward's personal household increased as he grew older, and in 1293 William of Blyborough took over as its administrator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Edward was probably given a religious education by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominican_friar" title="Dominican friar">Dominican friars</a>, whom his mother invited into his household in 1290.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> He was assigned one of his grandmother's followers, Guy Ferre, as his <i>magister</i>, who was responsible for his discipline, training him in riding and military skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> It is uncertain how well educated Edward was; there is little evidence for his ability to read and write, although his mother was keen that her other children were well educated, and Ferre was himself a relatively learned man for the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[nb 2]</a></sup> Edward likely mainly spoke <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_French" title="Anglo-Norman French">Anglo-Norman French</a> in his daily life, in addition to some English and possibly <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[nb 3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward had a normal upbringing for a member of a royal family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[nb 4]</a></sup> The prince was interested in horses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsebreeding" title="Horsebreeding">horsebreeding</a>, and became a good rider; he also liked dogs, in particular <a href="/wiki/Greyhound" title="Greyhound">greyhounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup> In his letters, he shows a quirky sense of humour, joking about sending unsatisfactory animals to his friends, such as horses who disliked carrying their riders, or lazy hunting dogs too slow to catch rabbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup> He was not particularly interested in <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a> or <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a>, both popular activities in the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup> He enjoyed music, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welsh_music" title="Welsh music">Welsh music</a> and the newly invented <a href="/wiki/Crwth" title="Crwth">crwth</a> instrument, as well as <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">musical organs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup> He did not take part in <a href="/wiki/Jousting" title="Jousting">jousting</a>, either because he lacked the aptitude or because he had been banned from participating for his personal safety, but he was certainly supportive of the sport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward grew up to be tall and muscular, and was considered good looking by the standards of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> He had a reputation as a competent public speaker and was known for his generosity to his household staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup> Unusually, he enjoyed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watercraft_rowing" title="Watercraft rowing">rowing</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Hedge" title="Hedge">hedging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ditch" title="Ditch">ditching</a>, and enjoyed associating with labourers and other lower-class workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[nb 5]</a></sup> This behaviour was not considered normal for the nobility of the period and attracted criticism from contemporaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1290, Edward's father had confirmed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Birgham" title="Treaty of Birgham">Treaty of Birgham</a>, in which he promised to marry his six-year-old son to the young <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret of Norway</a>, who had a potential claim to the crown of Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup> Margaret died later that year, bringing an end to the plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup> Edward's mother, Eleanor, died shortly afterwards, followed by his grandmother, Eleanor of Provence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P43_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P43-46">[41]</a></sup> Edward I was distraught at his wife's death and held a huge funeral for her; his son inherited the County of Ponthieu from Eleanor on her death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P43_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P43-46">[41]</a></sup> Next, a French marriage was considered for the young Edward, to help secure a lasting peace with France, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglo-French_War_of_1294-1303" title="Anglo-French War of 1294-1303">war</a> broke out in 1294.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP77HallamEverard_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP77HallamEverard-47">[42]</a></sup> The idea was replaced with the proposal of a marriage to a daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Guy,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Guy, Count of Flanders">Count of Flanders</a>, but this too failed after it was blocked by King <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP77HallamEverard_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP77HallamEverard-47">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_campaigns_in_Scotland">Early campaigns in Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early campaigns in Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:167px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edward_I_%26_II_Prince_of_Wales_1301.jpg"><img alt="Medieval painting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="165" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ab/Edward_I_%26_II_Prince_of_Wales_1301.jpg" width="165"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edward_I_%26_II_Prince_of_Wales_1301.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Early 14th-century depiction of Edward I (left) declaring his son Edward (right) the Prince of Wales</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between 1297 and 1298, Edward was left as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> in charge of England while the King campaigned in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a> against Philip IV, who had occupied part of the English King's lands in Gascony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup> On his return, Edward I signed a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1303)" title="Treaty of Paris (1303)">peace treaty</a>, under which he took Philip's sister, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_France,_Queen_of_England" title="Margaret of France, Queen of England">Margaret</a>, as his wife and agreed that Prince Edward would in due course marry Philip's daughter, <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella</a>, who was then only two years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> In theory, this marriage would mean that the disputed Duchy of Gascony would be inherited by a descendant of both Edward and Philip, providing a possible end to the long-running tensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup> The young Edward seems to have got on well with his new stepmother, who gave birth to Edward's two half-brothers, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_of_Brotherton,_1st_Earl_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas of Brotherton, 1st Earl of Norfolk">Thomas of Brotherton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Woodstock,_1st_Earl_of_Kent" title="Edmund of Woodstock, 1st Earl of Kent">Edmund of Woodstock</a>, in 1300 and 1301.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup> As king, Edward later provided his brothers with financial support and titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall2006PP198_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall2006PP198-52">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[nb 6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward I returned to Scotland once again in 1300, and this time took his son with him, making him the commander of the rearguard at the siege of <a href="/wiki/Caerlaverock_Castle" title="Caerlaverock Castle">Caerlaverock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup> In the spring of 1301, the King declared Edward the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>, granting him the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earldom_of_Chester" title="Earldom of Chester">earldom of Chester</a> and lands across North Wales; he seems to have hoped that this would help pacify the region, and that it would give his son some financial independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup> Edward received homage from his Welsh subjects and then joined his father for the 1301 Scottish campaign; he took an army of around 300 soldiers north with him and captured <a href="/wiki/Turnberry_Castle" title="Turnberry Castle">Turnberry Castle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup> Prince Edward also took part in the 1303 campaign during which he besieged <a href="/wiki/Brechin_Castle" title="Brechin Castle">Brechin Castle</a>, deploying his own siege engine in the operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup> In the spring of 1304, Edward conducted negotiations with the rebel Scottish leaders on the King's behalf and, when these failed, he joined his father for the siege of <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_94-95_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_94-95-58">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1305, Edward and his father quarrelled, probably over the issue of money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup> The Prince had an altercation with Bishop <a href="/wiki/Walter_Langton" title="Walter Langton">Walter Langton</a>, who served as the royal treasurer, apparently over the amount of financial support Edward received from the Crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_94-95_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_94-95-58">[52]</a></sup> Edward I defended his treasurer, and banished his son and his companions from his court, cutting off their financial support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup> After some negotiations involving family members and friends, the two men were reconciled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Scottish conflict flared up once again in 1306, when <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a> killed his rival <a href="/wiki/John_III_Comyn,_Lord_of_Badenoch" title="John III Comyn, Lord of Badenoch">John Comyn</a> and declared himself the King of the Scots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P199_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P199-62">[56]</a></sup> Edward I mobilised a fresh army, but decided that this time his son would be formally in charge of the expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P199_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P199-62">[56]</a></sup> Prince Edward was made the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Aquitaine" title="Duke of Aquitaine">Duke of Aquitaine</a> and then, along with many other young men, he was <a href="/wiki/Knight#Origins_of_medieval_knighthood" title="Knight">knighted</a> in a lavish ceremony at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Swans" title="Feast of the Swans">Feast of the Swans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup> Amid a huge feast in the neighbouring hall, reminiscent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthurian_legend" title="Arthurian legend">Arthurian legends</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">crusading events</a>, the assembly took a collective oath to defeat Bruce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup> It is unclear what role Prince Edward's forces played in the campaign that summer, which, under the orders of Edward I, saw a punitive, brutal retaliation against Bruce's faction in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[nb 7]</a></sup> Edward returned to England in September, where diplomatic negotiations to finalise a date for his wedding to Isabella continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Piers_Gaveston_and_sexuality">Piers Gaveston and sexuality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Piers Gaveston and sexuality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg"><img alt="Initial from a charter" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="880" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg/170px-Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg/255px-Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg/340px-Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gaveston_Cornwall_charter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Initial" title="Initial">Initial</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">charter</a> granting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piers_Gaveston" title="Piers Gaveston">Piers Gaveston</a> the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Earl of Cornwall">earldom of Cornwall</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During this time, Edward became close to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piers_Gaveston" title="Piers Gaveston">Piers Gaveston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[62]</a></sup> Gaveston was the son of one of the King's household knights whose lands lay adjacent to Gascony, and had himself joined Prince Edward's household in 1300, possibly on Edward I's instruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup> The two got on well; Gaveston became a <a href="/wiki/Squire" title="Squire">squire</a> and was soon being referred to as a close companion of Edward, before being knighted by the King during the Feast of Swans in 1306.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup> The King then exiled Gaveston to Gascony in 1307 for reasons that remain unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> According to one chronicler, Edward had asked his father to allow him to give Gaveston the County of Ponthieu, and the King responded furiously, pulling his son's hair out in great handfuls, before exiling Gaveston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> The official court records, however, show Gaveston being only temporarily exiled, supported by a comfortable stipend; no reason is given for the order, suggesting that it may have been an act aimed at punishing the prince in some way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The possibility that Edward had a sexual relationship with Gaveston or his later favourites has been extensively discussed by historians, complicated by the paucity of surviving evidence to determine for certain the details of their relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[nb 8]</a></sup> Homosexuality was fiercely condemned by the Church in 14th-century England, equating it with <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>, but engaging in sex with another man did not necessarily define an individual's personal identity in the same way that it might in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[70]</a></sup> Edward and Gaveston both had sexual relationships with their wives, who bore them children; Edward also had an illegitimate son, and may also have had an affair with his niece, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_de_Clare" title="Eleanor de Clare">Eleanor de Clare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The contemporary evidence supporting their homosexual relationship comes primarily from an anonymous chronicler in the 1320s who described how Edward "felt such love" for Gaveston that "he entered into a covenant of constancy, and bound himself with him before all other mortals with a bond of indissoluble love, firmly drawn up and fastened with a knot".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup> The first specific suggestion that Edward engaged in sex with men was recorded in 1334, when <a href="/wiki/Adam_Orleton" title="Adam Orleton">Adam Orleton</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Winchester" title="Bishop of Winchester">Bishop of Winchester</a>, was accused of having stated in 1326 that Edward was a "sodomite", although Orleton defended himself by arguing that he had meant that Edward's advisor, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Despenser_the_Younger" title="Hugh Despenser the Younger">Hugh Despenser the Younger</a>, was a sodomite, rather than the late King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[73]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meaux_Chronicle" title="Meaux Chronicle">Meaux Chronicle</a> from the 1390s simply notes that Edward gave himself "too much to the vice of sodomy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alternatively, Edward and Gaveston may have simply been friends with a close working relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> Contemporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronicler" title="Chronicler">chronicler</a> comments are vaguely worded; Orleton's allegations were at least in part politically motivated, and are very similar to the highly politicised sodomy allegations made against Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boniface_VIII" title="Boniface VIII">Boniface VIII</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">Knights Templar</a> in 1303 and 1308 respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup> Later accounts by chroniclers of Edward's activities may trace back to Orleton's original allegations, and were certainly adversely coloured by the events at the end of Edward's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> Such historians as Michael Prestwich and <a href="/wiki/J._R._S._Phillips" title="J. R. S. Phillips">Seymour Phillips</a> have argued that the public nature of the English royal court would have made it unlikely that any homosexual affairs would have remained discreet; neither the contemporary Church, Edward's father nor his father-in-law appear to have made any adverse comments about Edward's sexual behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>A more recent theory, proposed by the historian Pierre Chaplais, suggests that Edward and Gaveston entered into a bond of <a href="/wiki/Blood_brother" title="Blood brother">adoptive brotherhood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup> Compacts of adoptive brotherhood, in which the participants pledged to support each other in a form of "brotherhood-in-arms", were not unknown between close male friends in the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup> Many chroniclers described Edward and Gaveston's relationship as one of brotherhood, and one explicitly noted that Edward had taken Gaveston as his adopted brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[81]</a></sup> Chaplais argues that the pair may have made a formal compact in either 1300 or 1301, and that they would have seen any later promises they made to separate or to leave each other as having been made under duress, and therefore invalid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup> Such a compact, however, might not have excluded their relationship from having a sexual dimension as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestwich_2003_72_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich_2003_72-41">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_reign_.281307.E2.80.9311.29">Early reign (1307–11)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early reign (1307–11)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coronation_and_marriage">Coronation and marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Coronation and marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_(detail).jpg"><img alt="Picture of Edward II being crowned" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="944" data-file-width="957" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg/220px-Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg/330px-Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg/440px-Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edward_II_-_British_Library_Royal_20_A_ii_f10_(detail).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Edward II shown receiving the English crown in a contemporary illustration</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward I mobilised another army for the Scottish campaign in 1307, which Prince Edward was due to join that summer, but the elderly King had been increasingly unwell and died on 7 July at <a href="/wiki/Burgh_by_Sands" title="Burgh by Sands">Burgh by Sands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_123_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_123-91">[83]</a></sup> Edward travelled from London immediately after the news reached him, and on 20 July he was proclaimed king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP125_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP125-92">[84]</a></sup> He continued north into Scotland and on 4 August received homage from his Scottish supporters at <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>, before abandoning the campaign and returning south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP125_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP125-92">[84]</a></sup> Edward promptly recalled Piers Gaveston, who was then in exile, and appointed him as the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Earl of Cornwall">Earl of Cornwall</a>, before arranging his marriage to the wealthy Margaret de Clare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[nb 9]</a></sup> Edward also arrested his old adversary Bishop Langton, and dismissed him from his post as treasurer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[87]</a></sup> Edward I's body was kept at <a href="/wiki/Waltham_Abbey_Church" title="Waltham Abbey Church">Waltham Abbey</a> for several months before being taken for burial to Westminster, where Edward erected a simple <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> tomb for his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[nb 10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1308, Edward's marriage to Isabella of France proceeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[90]</a></sup> Edward crossed the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> to France in January, leaving Gaveston as his <i>custos regni</i> in charge of the kingdom in his absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[91]</a></sup> This arrangement was unusual, and involved unprecedented powers being delegated to Gaveston, backed by a specially engraved <a href="/wiki/Great_Seal_of_the_Realm" title="Great Seal of the Realm">Great Seal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[92]</a></sup> Edward probably hoped that the marriage would strengthen his position in Gascony and bring him much needed funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown1988P575_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brown1988P575-9">[9]</a></sup> The final negotiations, however, proved challenging: Edward and Philip IV did not like each other, and the French King drove a hard bargain over the size of Isabella's <a href="/wiki/Dower" title="Dower">dower</a> and the details of the administration of Edward's lands in France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[93]</a></sup> As part of the agreement, Edward gave homage to Philip for the Duchy of Aquitaine and agreed to a commission to complete the implementation of the 1303 Treaty of Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The pair were married in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boulogne" title="Boulogne">Boulogne</a> on 25 January.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[95]</a></sup> Edward gave Isabella a <a href="/wiki/Isabella_Psalter" title="Isabella Psalter">psalter</a> as a wedding gift, and her father gave her gifts worth over 21,000 <a href="/wiki/French_livre" title="French livre">livres</a> and a fragment of the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[96]</a></sup> The pair returned to England in February, where Edward had ordered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westminster_Palace" title="Westminster Palace">Westminster Palace</a> to be lavishly restored in readiness for their coronation and wedding feast, complete with marble tables, forty ovens and a fountain that produced wine and pimento, a spiced medieval drink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[97]</a></sup> After some delays, the ceremony went ahead on 25 February, under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Winchelsey" title="Robert Winchelsey">Robert Winchelsey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[98]</a></sup> As part of the coronation, Edward swore to uphold "the rightful laws and customs which the community of the realm shall have chosen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[99]</a></sup> It is uncertain what this meant: it might have been intended to force Edward to accept future legislation, it may have been inserted to prevent him from overturning any future vows he might take, or it may have been an attempt by the King to ingratiate himself with the barons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_140-143_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_140-143-110">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[nb 11]</a></sup> The event was marred by the large crowds of eager spectators who surged into the palace, knocking down a wall and forcing Edward to flee by the back door.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Isabella was only 12 years old at the time of her wedding, young by the standards of the period, and Edward probably had sexual relations with mistresses during their first few years together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HainesPhillipsP61_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-HainesPhillipsP61-113">[102]</a></sup> During this time Edward fathered an illegitimate son, <a href="/wiki/Adam_FitzRoy" title="Adam FitzRoy">Adam</a>, who was born possibly as early as 1307.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HainesPhillipsP61_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-HainesPhillipsP61-113">[102]</a></sup> Edward and Isabella's first son, the future <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, was born in 1312 amid great celebrations, and three more children followed: <a href="/wiki/John_of_Eltham,_Earl_of_Cornwall" title="John of Eltham, Earl of Cornwall">John</a> in 1316, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Woodstock" title="Eleanor of Woodstock">Eleanor</a> in 1318 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joan_of_The_Tower" title="Joan of The Tower">Joan</a> in 1321.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tensions_over_Gaveston">Tensions over Gaveston</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tensions over Gaveston">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philip_iv_and_family.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Philip V and family" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="600" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Philip_iv_and_family.jpg/220px-Philip_iv_and_family.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Philip_iv_and_family.jpg/330px-Philip_iv_and_family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Philip_iv_and_family.jpg/440px-Philip_iv_and_family.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philip_iv_and_family.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella of France</a>, third from left, with her father, <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>, centre</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gaveston's return from exile in 1307 was initially accepted by the barons, but opposition quickly grew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[104]</a></sup> He appeared to have an excessive influence on royal policy, leading to complaints from one chronicler that there were "two kings reigning in one kingdom, the one in name and the other in deed".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[105]</a></sup> Accusations, probably untrue, were levelled at Gaveston that he had stolen royal funds and had purloined Isabella's wedding presents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[106]</a></sup> Gaveston had played a key role at Edward's coronation, provoking fury from both the English and French contingents about the earl's ceremonial precedence and magnificent clothes, and about Edward's apparent preference for Gaveston's company over that of Isabella's at the feast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament</a> met in February 1308 in a heated atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP146_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP146-119">[108]</a></sup> Edward was eager to discuss the potential for governmental reform, but the barons were unwilling to begin any such debate until the problem of Gaveston had been resolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP146_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP146-119">[108]</a></sup> Violence seemed likely, but the situation was resolved through the mediation of the moderate <a href="/wiki/Henry_de_Lacy,_3rd_Earl_of_Lincoln" title="Henry de Lacy, 3rd Earl of Lincoln">Henry de Lacy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Lincoln" title="Earl of Lincoln">Earl of Lincoln</a>, who convinced the barons to back down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[109]</a></sup> A fresh parliament was held in April, where the barons once again criticised Gaveston, demanding his exile, this time supported by Isabella and the French monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[110]</a></sup> Edward resisted, but finally acquiesced, agreeing to send Gaveston to Aquitaine, under threat of <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> by the Archbishop of Canterbury should he return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[111]</a></sup> At the last moment, Edward changed his mind and instead sent Gaveston to <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, appointing him as the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lieutenant of Ireland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward called for a fresh military campaign for Scotland, but this idea was quietly abandoned, and instead the King and the barons met in August 1308 to discuss reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[113]</a></sup> Behind the scenes, Edward started negotiations to convince both <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_V" title="Pope Clement V">Pope Clement V</a> and Philip IV to allow Gaveston to return to England, offering in exchange to suppress the Knights Templar in England, and to release Bishop Langton from prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[114]</a></sup> Edward called a new meeting of members of the Church and key barons in January 1309, and the leading earls then gathered in March and April, possibly under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Thomas,_2nd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Thomas, 2nd Earl of Lancaster">Thomas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Earl of Lancaster">Earl of Lancaster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[115]</a></sup> Another parliament followed, which refused to allow Gaveston to return to England, but offered to grant Edward additional taxes if he agreed to a programme of reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward sent assurances to the Pope that the conflict surrounding Gaveston's role was at an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[117]</a></sup> On the basis of these promises, and procedural concerns about the how the original decision had been taken, the Pope agreed to annul the Archbishop's threat to excommunicate Gaveston, thus opening the possibility of Gaveston's return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[118]</a></sup> Gaveston arrived back in England in June, where he was met by Edward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[119]</a></sup> At the parliament the next month, Edward made a range of concessions to placate those opposed to Gaveston, including agreeing to limit the powers of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Steward" title="Lord Steward">royal steward</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Marshal" title="Marshal">marshal</a> of the royal household, to regulate the Crown's unpopular powers of <a href="/wiki/Purveyance" title="Purveyance">purveyance</a> and to abandon recently enacted customs legislation; in return, the parliament agreed to fresh taxes for the war in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[120]</a></sup> Temporarily, at least, Edward and the barons appeared to have come to a successful compromise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordinances_of_1311">Ordinances of 1311</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ordinances of 1311">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ordinances_of_1311" title="Ordinances of 1311">Ordinances of 1311</a></div>
<p>Following his return, Gaveston's relationship with the major barons became increasingly difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[122]</a></sup> He was considered arrogant, and he took to referring to the earls by offensive names, including calling one of their more powerful members the "dog of Warwick".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[123]</a></sup> The Earl of Lancaster and Gaveston's enemies refused to attend parliament in 1310 because Gaveston would be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[124]</a></sup> Edward was facing increasing financial problems, owing £22,000 to his <a href="/wiki/Frescobaldi" title="Frescobaldi">Frescobaldi</a> Italian bankers, and facing protests about how he was using his right of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prise" title="Prise">prises</a> to acquire supplies for the war in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[125]</a></sup> His attempts to raise an army for Scotland collapsed and the earls suspended the collection of the new taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The King and parliament met again in February 1310, and the proposed discussions of Scottish policy were replaced by debate of domestic problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[127]</a></sup> Edward was petitioned to abandon Gaveston as his counsellor and instead adopt the advice of 21 elected barons, termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordainers" title="Ordainers">Ordainers</a>, who would carry out a widespread reform of both the government and the royal household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[128]</a></sup> Under huge pressure, Edward agreed to the proposal and the Ordainers were elected, broadly evenly split between reformers and conservatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[129]</a></sup> While the Ordainers began their plans for reform, Edward and Gaveston took a new army of around 4,700 men to Scotland, where the military situation had continued to deteriorate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[130]</a></sup> Robert the Bruce declined to give battle and the campaign progressed ineffectually over the winter until supplies and money ran out in 1311, forcing Edward to return south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>By now the Ordainers had drawn up their Ordinances for reform and Edward had little political choice but to give way and accept them in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[132]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ordinances_of_1311" title="Ordinances of 1311">Ordinances of 1311</a> contained clauses limiting the King's right to go to war or to grant land without parliament's approval, giving parliament control over the royal administration, abolishing the system of prises, excluding the Frescobaldi bankers, and introducing a system to monitor the adherence to the Ordinances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[133]</a></sup> In addition, the Ordinances exiled Gaveston once again, this time with instructions that he should not be allowed to live anywhere within Edward's lands, including Gascony and Ireland, and that he should be stripped of his titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[134]</a></sup> Edward retreated to his estates at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kings_Langley" title="Kings Langley">Kings Langley</a>; Gaveston left England, possibly for northern France or Flanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-reign_.281311.E2.80.9321.29">Mid-reign (1311–21)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mid-reign (1311–21)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_Gaveston">Death of Gaveston</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Death of Gaveston">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Tensions between Edward and the barons remained high, and the earls opposed to the King kept their personal armies mobilised late into 1311.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[136]</a></sup> By now Edward had become estranged from his cousin, the Earl of Lancaster, who held the earldoms of Lancaster, <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Leicester" title="Earl of Leicester">Leicester</a>, Lincoln, <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Earl of Salisbury">Salisbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Derby" title="Earl of Derby">Derby</a>, with an income of around £11,000 a year from his lands, almost double that of the next wealthiest baron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[137]</a></sup> Backed by the earls of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_FitzAlan,_9th_Earl_of_Arundel" title="Edmund FitzAlan, 9th Earl of Arundel">Arundel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_de_Clare,_8th_Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Gilbert de Clare, 8th Earl of Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_de_Bohun,_4th_Earl_of_Hereford" title="Humphrey de Bohun, 4th Earl of Hereford">Hereford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aymer_de_Valence,_2nd_Earl_of_Pembroke" title="Aymer de Valence, 2nd Earl of Pembroke">Pembroke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guy_de_Beauchamp,_10th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Guy de Beauchamp, 10th Earl of Warwick">Warwick</a>, Lancaster led a powerful faction in England, but he was not personally interested in practical administration, nor was he a particularly imaginative or effective politician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward responded to the baronial threat by revoking the Ordinances and recalling Gaveston to England, being reunited with him at York in January 1312.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[139]</a></sup> The barons were furious and met in London, where Gaveston was excommunicated by the Archbishop of Canterbury and plans were put in place to capture Gaveston and prevent him from fleeing to Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[140]</a></sup> Edward, Isabella and Gaveston left for Newcastle, pursued by Lancaster and his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[141]</a></sup> Abandoning many of their belongings, the royal party fled by ship and landed at <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough</a>, where Gaveston stayed while Edward and Isabella returned to York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[142]</a></sup> After a short siege, Gaveston surrendered to the earls of Pembroke and <a href="/wiki/John_de_Warenne,_7th_Earl_of_Surrey" title="John de Warenne, 7th Earl of Surrey">Surrey</a>, on the promise that he would not be harmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[143]</a></sup> He had with him a huge collection of gold, silver and gems, probably part of the royal treasury, which he was later accused of having stolen from Edward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the way back from the north, Pembroke stopped in the village of <a href="/wiki/Deddington" title="Deddington">Deddington</a> in the Midlands, putting Gaveston under guard there while he went to visit his wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[145]</a></sup> The Earl of Warwick took this opportunity to seize Gaveston, taking him to <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Castle" title="Warwick Castle">Warwick Castle</a>, where Lancaster and the rest of his faction assembled on 18 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[146]</a></sup> At a brief trial, Gaveston was declared guilty of being a traitor under the terms of the Ordinances; he was executed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blacklow_Hill" title="Blacklow Hill">Blacklow Hill</a> the following day, under the authority of the Earl of Lancaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[147]</a></sup> Gaveston's body was not buried until 1315, when his funeral was held in <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Langley_Priory" title="King's Langley Priory">King's Langley Priory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tensions_with_Lancaster_and_France">Tensions with Lancaster and France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Tensions with Lancaster and France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trojka_kralove.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Edward at a knighting ceremony" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="314" data-file-width="386" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trojka_kralove.jpg/220px-Trojka_kralove.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Trojka_kralove.jpg/330px-Trojka_kralove.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Trojka_kralove.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trojka_kralove.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Edward (left) and Philip IV at the knighting ceremony of <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame</a>, 1312</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Reactions to the death of Gaveston varied considerably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaplais_1994_89_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaplais_1994_89-160">[149]</a></sup> Edward was furious and deeply upset over what he saw as the murder of Gaveston; he made provisions for Gaveston's family, and intended to take revenge on the barons involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[150]</a></sup> The earls of Pembroke and Surrey were embarrassed and angry about Warwick's actions, and shifted their support to Edward in the aftermath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[151]</a></sup> To Lancaster and his core of supporters, the execution had been both legal and necessary to preserve the stability of the kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaplais_1994_89_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chaplais_1994_89-160">[149]</a></sup> Civil war again appeared likely, but in December, the Earl of Pembroke negotiated a potential peace treaty between the two sides, which would pardon the opposition barons for the killing of Gaveston, in exchange for their support for a fresh campaign in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[152]</a></sup> Lancaster and Warwick, however, did not give the treaty their immediate approval, and further negotiations continued through most of 1313.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, the Earl of Pembroke had been negotiating with France to resolve the long-standing disagreements over the administration of Gascony, and as part of this Edward and Isabella agreed to travel to Paris in June 1313 to meet with Philip IV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[154]</a></sup> Edward probably hoped both to resolve the problems in the south of France and to win Philip's support in the dispute with the barons; for Philip it was an opportunity to impress his son-in-law with his power and wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[155]</a></sup> It proved a spectacular visit, including a grand ceremony in which the two kings knighted Philip's sons and 200 other men in <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre Dame de Paris">Notre Dame</a>, large banquets along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Seine" title="River Seine">River Seine</a>, and a public declaration that both kings and their queens would join a crusade to the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[156]</a></sup> Philip gave lenient terms for settling the problems in Gascony, and the event was spoiled only by a serious fire in Edward's quarters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>On his return from France, Edward found his political position greatly strengthened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[158]</a></sup> After intense negotiation, the earls, including Lancaster and Warwick, came to a compromise in October 1313, fundamentally very similar to the draft agreement of the previous December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[159]</a></sup> Edward's finances improved, thanks to parliament agreeing to the raising of taxes, a loan of 160,000 <a href="/wiki/Florin" title="Florin">florins</a> (£25,000) from the Pope, £33,000 that he borrowed from Philip, and further loans organised by Edward's new Italian banker, Antonio Pessagno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[160]</a></sup> For the first time in his reign, Edward's government was well-funded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Battle of Bannockburn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg"><img alt="Sketch of the Battle of Bannockburb" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="799" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg/170px-Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg/255px-Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg/340px-Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_from_Holkham_Bible.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314 from the Holkham Bible</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a></div>
<p>By 1314, Robert the Bruce had recaptured most of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_castles" title="Scottish castles">castles in Scotland</a> once held by Edward, pushing raiding parties into northern England as far as <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">Carlisle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP223_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP223-173">[162]</a></sup> In response, Edward planned a major military campaign with the support of Lancaster and the barons, mustering a large army between 15,000 and 20,000 strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[163]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Robert had besieged Stirling Castle, a key fortification in Scotland; its English commander had stated that unless Edward arrived by 24 June, he would surrender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP223_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP223-173">[162]</a></sup> News of this reached the King in late May, and he decided to speed up his march north from <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Berwick</a> to relieve the castle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[164]</a></sup> Robert, with between 5,500 and 6,500 troops, predominantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spearmen" title="Spearmen">spearmen</a>, prepared to prevent Edward's forces from reaching Stirling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>The battle began on 23 June as the English army attempted to force its way across the high ground of the <a href="/wiki/Bannock_Burn" title="Bannock Burn">Bannock Burn</a>, which was surrounded by marshland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P230_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P230-177">[166]</a></sup> Skirmishing between the two sides broke out, resulting in the death of Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_de_Bohun" title="Henry de Bohun">Henry de Bohun</a>, whom Robert killed in personal combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P230_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P230-177">[166]</a></sup> Edward continued his advance the following day, and encountered the bulk of the Scottish army as they emerged from the woods of New Park.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_231-232_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_231-232-178">[167]</a></sup> Edward appears not to have expected the Scots to give battle here, and as a result had kept his forces in marching, rather than battle, order, with the <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a> − who would usually have been used to break up enemy spear formations − at the back of his army, rather than the front.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_231-232_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_231-232-178">[167]</a></sup> His cavalry found it hard to operate in the cramped terrain and were crushed by Robert's spearmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P232_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P232-179">[168]</a></sup> The English army was overwhelmed and its leaders were unable to regain control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P232_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P232-179">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward stayed behind to fight, but it became obvious to the Earl of Pembroke that the battle was lost and he dragged the King away from the battlefield, hotly pursued by the Scottish forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P233_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P233-180">[169]</a></sup> Edward only just escaped the heavy fighting, making a vow to found a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carmelite" title="Carmelite">Carmelite</a> religious house at <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> if he survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P233_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P233-180">[169]</a></sup> The historian Roy Haines describes the defeat as a "calamity of stunning proportions" for the English, whose losses in the battle were huge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[170]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the defeat, Edward retreated to <a href="/wiki/Dunbar" title="Dunbar">Dunbar</a>, then travelled by ship to Berwick, and then back to <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>; in his absence, Stirling Castle quickly fell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Famine_and_criticism">Famine and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Famine and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%9317" title="Great Famine of 1315–17">Great Famine of 1315–17</a></div>
<p>After the fiasco of Bannockburn, the earls of Lancaster and Warwick saw their political influence increase, and they pressured Edward to re-implement the Ordinances of 1311.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[172]</a></sup> Lancaster became the head of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_council" title="Royal council">royal council</a> in 1316, promising to take forward the Ordinances through a new reform commission, but he appears to have abandoned this role soon afterwards, partially because of disagreements with the other barons, and possibly because of ill-health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[173]</a></sup> Lancaster refused to meet with Edward in parliament for the next two years, bringing effective governance to a standstill. This stymied any hopes for a fresh campaign into Scotland and raised fears of civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[174]</a></sup> After much negotiation, once again involving the Earl of Pembroke, Edward and Lancaster finally agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Leake" title="Treaty of Leake">Treaty of Leake</a> in August 1318, which pardoned Lancaster and his faction and established a new royal council, temporarily averting conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward's difficulties were exacerbated by prolonged problems in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_English_Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages#The_Great_Famine_of_1315.E2.80.931317" title="Economics of English Agriculture in the Middle Ages">English agriculture</a>, part of a wider phenomenon in northern Europe known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%9317" title="Great Famine of 1315–17">Great Famine</a>. It began with torrential rains in late 1314, followed by a very cold winter and heavy rains the following spring that killed many sheep and cattle. The bad weather continued, almost unabated, into 1321, resulting in a string of bad harvests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[176]</a></sup> Revenues from the exports of wool plummeted and the price of food rose, despite attempts by Edward II's government to control prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup> Edward called for hoarders to release food, and tried to encourage both internal trade and the importation of grain, but with little success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup> The requisitioning of provisions for the royal court during the famine years only added to tensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[179]</a></sup></p>
