<p>Meanwhile, Robert the Bruce exploited his victory at Bannockburn to raid northern England, initially attacking Carlisle and Berwick, and then reaching further south into <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, even threatening York itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[180]</a></sup> Edward undertook an expensive but unsuccessful campaign to stem the advance in 1319, but the famine made it increasingly difficult to keep his garrisons supplied with food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[181]</a></sup> Meanwhile, a Scottish expedition led by Robert's brother successfully invaded Ireland in 1315, where <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bruce" title="Edward Bruce">Edward Bruce</a> declared himself the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Ireland" title="King of Ireland">King of Ireland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[182]</a></sup> He was finally defeated in 1318 by Edward II's Irish justiciar, Edmund Butler, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Faughart" title="Battle of Faughart">Battle of Faughart</a>, and Edward Bruce's severed head was sent back to the King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[183]</a></sup> Revolts also broke out <a href="/wiki/Banastre_Rebellion" title="Banastre Rebellion">in Lancashire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> in 1315, and in <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan" title="Glamorgan">Glamorgan</a> in Wales in 1316, but were suppressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>The famine and the Scottish policy were felt to be a punishment from God, and complaints about Edward multiplied, one contemporary poem describing the "Evil Times of Edward II".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[185]</a></sup> Many criticised Edward's "improper" and ignoble interest in rural pursuits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[186]</a></sup> In 1318, a mentally ill man named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_Powderham" title="John of Powderham">John of Powderham</a> appeared in Oxford, claiming that he was the real Edward II, and that Edward was a <a href="/wiki/Changeling" title="Changeling">changeling</a>, swapped at birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[187]</a></sup> John was duly executed, but his claims resonated with those criticising Edward for his lack of regal behaviour and steady leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[188]</a></sup> Opposition also grew around Edward's treatment of his royal favourites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>He had managed to retain some of his previous advisers, despite attempts by the Ordainers to remove them, and divided the extensive <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_de_Clare,_8th_Earl_of_Gloucester#Dispersal_of_estates_and_aftermath" title="Gilbert de Clare, 8th Earl of Gloucester">de Clare inheritance</a> among two of his new favourites, the former household knights Hugh Audley and <a href="/wiki/Roger_d%27Amory" title="Roger d'Amory">Roger Damory</a>, instantly making them extremely rich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[nb 12]</a></sup> Many of the moderates who had helped deliver the peaceful compromise in 1318 now began to turn against Edward, making violence ever more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_reign_.281321.E2.80.9326.29">Later reign (1321–26)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Later reign (1321–26)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Despenser_War">The Despenser War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The Despenser War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Despenser_War" title="The Despenser War">The Despenser War</a></div>
<p>The long-threatened <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Despenser_War" title="The Despenser War">civil war</a> finally broke out in England in 1321,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[193]</a></sup> triggered by the tension between many of the barons and the royal favourites, the Despenser family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[194]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_Despenser_the_Elder" title="Hugh Despenser the Elder">Hugh Despenser the Elder</a> had served both Edward and his father, while Hugh Despenser the Younger had married into the wealthy de Clare family, become the King's <a href="/wiki/Chamberlain_(office)" title="Chamberlain (office)">chamberlain</a>, and acquired Glamorgan in the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Marches" title="Welsh Marches">Welsh Marches</a> in 1317.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[195]</a></sup> Hugh the Younger subsequently expanded his holdings and power across Wales, mainly at the expense of the other <a href="/wiki/Marcher_Lord" title="Marcher Lord">Marcher Lords</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[196]</a></sup> The Earl of Lancaster and the Despensers were fierce enemies, and Lancaster's antipathy was shared by most of the Despensers' neighbours, including the Earl of Hereford, the <a href="/wiki/Mortimer#Medieval_magnates" title="Mortimer">Mortimer family</a> and the recently elevated Hugh Audley and Roger Damory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[197]</a></sup> Edward, however, increasingly relied on the Despensers for advice and support, and he was particularly close to Hugh the Younger, whom one chronicler noted he "loved ... dearly with all his heart and mind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1321, Lancaster mobilised a coalition of the Despensers' enemies across the Marcher territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[199]</a></sup> Edward and Hugh the Younger became aware of these plans in March and headed west, hoping that negotiations led by the moderate Earl of Pembroke would defuse the crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[200]</a></sup> This time, Pembroke made his excuses and declined to intervene, and war broke out in May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[201]</a></sup> The Despensers' lands were quickly seized by a coalition of the Marcher Lords and the local gentry, and Lancaster held a high-level gathering of the barons and clergy in June which condemned the Despensers for having broken the Ordinances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[202]</a></sup> Edward attempted reconciliation, but in July the opposition occupied London and called for the permanent removal of the Despensers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[203]</a></sup> Fearing that he might be deposed if he refused, Edward agreed to exile the Despensers and pardoned the Marcher Lords for their actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[204]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward began to plan his revenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[205]</a></sup> With the help of Pembroke, the King formed a small coalition of his half-brothers, a few of the earls and some of the senior clergy, and prepared for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[206]</a></sup> Edward started with <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_de_Badlesmere,_1st_Baron_Badlesmere" title="Bartholomew de Badlesmere, 1st Baron Badlesmere">Bartholomew of Badlesmere</a>, and Isabella was sent to Bartholomew's stronghold, <a href="/wiki/Leeds_Castle" title="Leeds Castle">Leeds Castle</a>, to deliberately create a <i><a href="/wiki/Casus_belli" title="Casus belli">casus belli</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P397_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P397-219">[207]</a></sup> Bartholomew's wife, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_de_Clare,_Baroness_Badlesmere" title="Margaret de Clare, Baroness Badlesmere">Margaret</a>, took the bait and her men killed several of Isabella's retinue, giving Edward an excuse to intervene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P397_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P397-219">[207]</a></sup> Lancaster refused to help Bartholomew, who was his personal enemy, and Edward quickly regained control of south-east England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[208]</a></sup> Alarmed, Lancaster now mobilised his own army in the north of England, and Edward mustered his own forces in the south-west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[209]</a></sup> The Despensers returned from exile and were pardoned by the royal council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December, Edward led his army across the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">River Severn</a> and advanced into the Welsh Marches, where the opposition forces had gathered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[211]</a></sup> The coalition of Marcher Lords crumbled and the Mortimers surrendered to the King,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[212]</a></sup> but Damory, Audley and the Earl of Hereford marched north in January to join Lancaster, who had laid siege the King's castle at <a href="/wiki/Tickhill_Castle" title="Tickhill Castle">Tickhill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP406407_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP406407-225">[213]</a></sup> Bolstered by fresh reinforcements from the Marcher Lords, Edward pursued them, meeting Lancaster's army on 10 March at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burton-on-Trent" title="Burton-on-Trent">Burton-on-Trent</a>. Lancaster, outnumbered, retreated without a fight, fleeing north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP406407_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP406407-225">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Harclay,_1st_Earl_of_Carlisle" title="Andrew Harclay, 1st Earl of Carlisle">Andrew Harclay</a> cornered Lancaster at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Boroughbridge" title="Battle of Boroughbridge">Battle of Boroughbridge</a>, and captured the Earl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[214]</a></sup> Edward and Hugh the Younger met Lancaster at <a href="/wiki/Pontefract_Castle" title="Pontefract Castle">Pontefract</a>, where, after a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summary_trial" title="Summary trial">summary trial</a>, the earl was found guilty of treason and beheaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_and_the_Despensers">Edward and the Despensers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Edward and the Despensers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg"><img alt="painting of Edward hunting" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="500" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg/220px-Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg/330px-Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg/440px-Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philippe4_eduard2_ludvikNavarra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Edward (third from the left) hunting with Philip IV</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward punished Lancaster's supporters through a system of special courts across the country, with the judges instructed in advance how to sentence the accused, who were not allowed to speak in their own defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[216]</a></sup> Many of these so-called "Contrariants" were simply executed, and others were imprisoned or fined, with their lands seized and their surviving relatives detained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[217]</a></sup> The Earl of Pembroke, whom Edward now mistrusted, was arrested and only released after pledging all of his possessions as <a href="/wiki/Collateral_(finance)" title="Collateral (finance)">collateral</a> for his own loyalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[218]</a></sup> Edward was able to reward his loyal supporters, especially the Despenser family, with the confiscated estates and new titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[219]</a></sup> The fines and confiscations made Edward rich: almost £15,000 was brought in during the first few months, and by 1326, Edward's treasury contained £62,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[220]</a></sup> A parliament was held at York in March 1322 at which the Ordinances were formally revoked through the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_York" title="Statute of York">Statute of York</a>, and fresh taxes agreed for a new campaign against the Scots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>The English campaign against Scotland was planned on a massive scale, with a force of around 23,350 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP426_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP426-234">[222]</a></sup> Edward advanced through <a href="/wiki/Lothian" title="Lothian">Lothian</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, but Robert the Bruce declined to meet him in battle, drawing Edward further into Scotland. Plans to resupply the campaign by sea failed, and the large army rapidly ran out of food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP426_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP426-234">[222]</a></sup> Edward was forced to retreat south of the border, pursued by Scottish raiding parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011PP426_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011PP426-234">[222]</a></sup> Edward's illegitimate son, Adam, died during the campaign, and the raiding parties almost captured Isabella, who was staying at <a href="/wiki/Tynemouth" title="Tynemouth">Tynemouth</a> and was forced to flee by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[223]</a></sup> The King planned a fresh campaign, backed by a round of further taxes, but confidence in Edward's Scottish policy was diminishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[224]</a></sup> Andrew Harclay, instrumental in securing Edward's victories the previous year and recently made the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Carlisle" title="Earl of Carlisle">Earl of Carlisle</a>, independently negotiated a peace treaty with Robert the Bruce, proposing that Edward would recognise Robert as the King of Scotland and that, in return, Robert would cease to interfere in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[225]</a></sup> Edward was furious and immediately executed Harclay, but agreed to a thirteen-year truce with Robert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hugh Despenser the Younger lived and ruled in grand style, playing a leading role in Edward's government, and executing policy through a wide network of family retainers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[227]</a></sup> Supported by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Baldock" title="Robert Baldock">Robert Baldock</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_Stapledon" title="Walter Stapledon">Walter Stapledon</a>, Edward's chancellor and lord treasurer, the Despensers accumulated land and wealth, using their position in government to provide superficial cover for what historian Seymour Phillips describes as "the reality of fraud, threats of violence and abuse of legal procedure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[228]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Edward faced growing opposition. <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">Miracles</a> were reported around the late Earl of Lancaster's tomb, and at the <a href="/wiki/Gallows" title="Gallows">gallows</a> used to execute members of the opposition in Bristol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[229]</a></sup> Law and order began to break down, encouraged by the chaos caused by the seizure of lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[230]</a></sup> The old opposition consisting of Marcher Lords' associates attempted to free the prisoners Edward held in <a href="/wiki/Wallingford_Castle" title="Wallingford Castle">Wallingford Castle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Mortimer,_1st_Earl_of_March" title="Roger Mortimer, 1st Earl of March">Roger Mortimer</a>, one of the most prominent of the imprisoned Marcher Lords, escaped from the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> and fled to France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[231]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_France">War with France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: War with France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/War_of_Saint-Sardos" title="War of Saint-Sardos">War of Saint-Sardos</a></div>
<p>The disagreements between Edward and the French Crown over the Duchy of Gascony led to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Saint-Sardos" title="War of Saint-Sardos">War of Saint-Sardos</a> in 1324.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[232]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV_of_France" title="Charles IV of France">Charles</a>, Edward's brother-in-law, had become King of France in 1322, and was more aggressive than his predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[233]</a></sup> In 1323, he insisted that Edward come to Paris to give homage for Gascony, and demanded that Edward's administrators in Gascony allow French officials there to carry out orders given in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[234]</a></sup> Matters came to a head in October when a group of Edward's soldiers hanged a French sergeant for attempting to build a new <a href="/wiki/Bastide" title="Bastide">fortified town</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Agenais" title="Agenais">Agenais</a>, a contested section of the Gascon border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[235]</a></sup> Edward denied any responsibility for this incident, but relations between Edward and Charles soured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[236]</a></sup> In 1324, Edward dispatched the Earl of Pembroke to Paris to broker a solution, but the earl died suddenly of an illness along the way. Charles mobilised his army and ordered the invasion of Gascony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward's forces in Gascony were around 4,400 strong, but the French army, commanded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_of_Valois" title="Charles of Valois">Charles of Valois</a>, numbered 7,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P464_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P464-250">[238]</a></sup> Valois took the Agenais and then advanced further and cut off the main city of <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux" title="Bordeaux">Bordeaux</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P464_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P464-250">[238]</a></sup> In response, Edward ordered the arrest of any French persons in England and seized Isabella's lands, on the basis that she was of French origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[239]</a></sup> In November 1324 he met with the earls and the English Church, who recommended that Edward should lead a force of 11,000 men to Gascony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[240]</a></sup> Edward decided not to go personally, sending instead the Earl of Surrey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[241]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Edward opened up fresh negotiations with the French king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[242]</a></sup> Charles advanced various proposals, the most tempting of which was the suggestion that if Isabella and Prince Edward were to travel to Paris, and the Prince was to give homage to the French King for Gascony, he would terminate the war and return the Agenais.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[243]</a></sup> Edward and his advisers had concerns about sending the prince to France, but agreed to send Isabella on her own as an envoy in March 1325.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[244]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_from_power_.281326.E2.80.9327.29">Fall from power (1326–27)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Fall from power (1326–27)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rift_with_Isabella">Rift with Isabella</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Rift with Isabella">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isabela_Karel_Eda.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Edward III giving homage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="315" data-file-width="247" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Isabela_Karel_Eda.jpg/170px-Isabela_Karel_Eda.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Isabela_Karel_Eda.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isabela_Karel_Eda.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The future <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> giving homage in 1325 to Charles IV under the guidance of Isabella of France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Isabella, with Edward's envoys, carried out negotiations with the French in late March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P472_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P472-257">[245]</a></sup> The negotiations proved difficult, and they arrived at a settlement only after Isabella personally intervened with her brother, Charles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P472_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P472-257">[245]</a></sup> The terms favoured the French crown: in particular, Edward would give homage in person to Charles for Gascony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[246]</a></sup> Concerned about the consequences of war breaking out once again, Edward agreed to the treaty but decided to give Gascony to his son, Edward, and sent the prince to give homage in Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[247]</a></sup> The young Prince Edward crossed the English Channel and completed the bargain in September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[nb 13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward now expected Isabella and their son to return to England, but instead she remained in France and showed no intention of making her way back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[250]</a></sup> Until 1322, Edward and Isabella's marriage appears to have been successful, but by the time the Queen left for France in 1325, it had deteriorated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[251]</a></sup> Isabella appears to have disliked Hugh Despenser the Younger intensely, not least because of his abuse of high-status women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[252]</a></sup> Isabella was embarrassed that she had fled from Scottish armies three times during her marriage to Edward, and she blamed Hugh for the final occurrence in 1322.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[253]</a></sup> When Edward had negotiated the recent truce with Robert the Bruce, he had severely disadvantaged a range of noble families who owned land in Scotland, including the Beaumonts, close friends of Isabella's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[254]</a></sup> She was also angry about the arrest of her household and seizure of her lands in 1324. Finally, Edward had taken away her children and given the custody of them to Hugh Despenser's wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>By February 1326, it was clear that Isabella was involved in a relationship with the exiled Marcher Lord, Roger Mortimer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[256]</a></sup> It is unclear when Isabella first met Mortimer or when their relationship began, but they both wanted to see Edward and the Despensers removed from power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[nb 14]</a></sup> The King appealed for his son to return, and for Charles to intervene on his behalf, but this had no effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward's opponents began to gather around Isabella and Mortimer in Paris, and Edward became increasingly anxious about the possibility that Mortimer might invade England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[260]</a></sup> Isabella and Mortimer turned to <a href="/wiki/William_I,_Count_of_Hainaut" title="William I, Count of Hainaut">William</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Hainaut" title="Count of Hainaut">Count of Hainaut</a>, and proposed a marriage between Prince Edward and William's daughter, <a href="/wiki/Philippa_of_Hainault" title="Philippa of Hainault">Philippa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[261]</a></sup> In return for the advantageous alliance with the English heir to the throne, and a sizeable dower for the bride, William offered 132 <a href="/wiki/Ship_transport" title="Ship transport">transport vessels</a> and 8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warships" title="Warships">warships</a> to assist in the invasion of England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[262]</a></sup> Prince Edward and Philippa were betrothed on 27 August, and Isabella and Mortimer prepared for their campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[263]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion">Invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_England_(1326)" title="Invasion of England (1326)">Invasion of England (1326)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of replica Oxwich Brooch" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="674" data-file-width="690" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg/170px-Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg/255px-Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg/340px-Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Replica_of_Oxwich_Brooch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replica of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Oxwich_Brooch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Oxwich Brooch (page does not exist)">Oxwich Brooch</a>, probably owned by Edward and looted during the events of 1326<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[264]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During August and September 1326, Edward mobilised his defences along the coasts of England to protect against the possibility of an invasion either by France or by Roger Mortimer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[265]</a></sup> Fleets were gathered at the ports of <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a> in the south and <a href="/wiki/River_Orwell" title="River Orwell">Orwell</a> on the east coast, and a raiding force of 1,600 men was sent across the English Channel into <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a> as a diversionary attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[266]</a></sup> Edward issued a nationalistic appeal for his subjects to defend the kingdom, but with little impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[267]</a></sup> The regime's hold on power at the local level was fragile, the Despensers were widely disliked, and many of those Edward entrusted with the defence of the kingdom proved incompetent, or promptly turned against the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[268]</a></sup> Some 2,000 men were ordered to gather at Orwell to repel any invasion, but only 55 appear to have actually arrived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[269]</a></sup></p>
<p>Roger Mortimer, Isabella, and thirteen-year-old Prince Edward, accompanied by King Edward's half-brother Edmund of Woodstock, landed in Orwell on 24 September with a small force of men and met with no resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[270]</a></sup> Instead, enemies of the Despensers moved rapidly to join them, including Edward's other half-brother, Thomas of Brotherton; <a href="/wiki/Henry,_3rd_Earl_of_Lancaster" title="Henry, 3rd Earl of Lancaster">Henry of Lancaster</a>, who had inherited the earldom from his brother Thomas; and a range of senior clergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[271]</a></sup> Ensconced in the residence halls of the fortified and secure <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>, Edward attempted to garner support from within the capital. The city of London rose against his government, and on 2 October the King left London, taking the Despensers with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[272]</a></sup> London descended into anarchy, as mobs attacked Edward's remaining officials and associates, killing his former treasurer Walter Stapledon in <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a>, and taking the Tower and releasing the prisoners inside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward continued west up the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley" title="Thames Valley">Thames Valley</a>, reaching Gloucester between 9 and 12 October; he hoped to reach Wales and from there mobilise an army against the invaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[274]</a></sup> Mortimer and Isabella were not far behind. Proclamations condemned the Despensers' recent regime. Day-by-day they gathered new supporters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[275]</a></sup> Edward and the younger Despenser crossed over the border and set sail from <a href="/wiki/Chepstow" title="Chepstow">Chepstow</a>, probably aiming first for <a href="/wiki/Lundy" title="Lundy">Lundy</a> and then for Ireland, where the King hoped to receive refuge and raise a fresh army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[276]</a></sup> Bad weather drove them back, though, and they landed at <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a>. Edward retreated to <a href="/wiki/Caerphilly_Castle" title="Caerphilly Castle">Caerphilly Castle</a> and attempted to rally his remaining forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[277]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward's authority collapsed in England where, in the King's absence, Isabella's faction took over the administration with the support of the Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[278]</a></sup> Her forces surrounded Bristol, where Hugh Despenser the Elder had taken shelter; he surrendered and was promptly executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[279]</a></sup> Edward and Hugh the Younger fled their castle around 2 November, leaving behind jewellery, considerable supplies and at least £13,000 in cash, possibly once again hoping to reach Ireland, but on 16 November they were betrayed and captured by a search party north of Caerphilly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[280]</a></sup> Edward was escorted first to <a href="/wiki/Monmouth_Castle" title="Monmouth Castle">Monmouth Castle</a>, and from there back into England, where he was held at Henry of Lancaster's fortress at <a href="/wiki/Kenilworth_Castle" title="Kenilworth Castle">Kenilworth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[281]</a></sup> The King's final remaining forces, by now besieged in Caerphilly Castle, surrendered after five months in April 1327.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[282]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abdication">Abdication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Abdication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eduard2_arest.jpg"><img alt="Painting of Isabella capturing Edward" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="479" data-file-width="430" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Eduard2_arest.jpg/170px-Eduard2_arest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Eduard2_arest.jpg/255px-Eduard2_arest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Eduard2_arest.jpg/340px-Eduard2_arest.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eduard2_arest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An 15th-century depiction of Isabella capturing Edward</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Isabella and Mortimer rapidly took revenge on the former regime. Hugh Despenser the Younger was put on trial, declared a traitor and sentenced to be drawn, disembowelled, castrated and quartered; he was duly executed on 24 November 1326.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[283]</a></sup> Edward's former chancellor, Robert Baldock, died in <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Prison" title="Fleet Prison">Fleet Prison</a>; the Earl of Arundel was beheaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[284]</a></sup> Edward's position, however, was problematic; he was still married to Isabella and, in principle, he remained the king, but most of the new administration had a lot to lose were he to be released and potentially regain power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[285]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was no established procedure for removing an English king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[286]</a></sup> Adam Orleton, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Hereford" title="Bishop of Hereford">Bishop of Hereford</a>, made a series of public allegations about Edward's conduct as king, and in January 1327 a parliament convened at Westminster at which the question of Edward's future was raised; Edward refused to attend the gathering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[287]</a></sup> Parliament, initially ambivalent, responded to the London crowds that called for Prince Edward to take the throne. On 12 January the leading barons and clergy agreed that Edward II should be removed and replaced by his son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[288]</a></sup> The following day it was presented to an assembly of the barons, where it was argued that Edward's weak leadership and personal faults had led the kingdom into disaster, and that he was incompetent to lead the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[289]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after this, a representative delegation of barons, clergy and knights was sent to Kenilworth to speak to the King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[290]</a></sup> On 20 January 1327, Henry of Lancaster and the <a href="/wiki/John_de_Stratford" title="John de Stratford">bishops of Winchester</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Burghersh" title="Henry Burghersh">Lincoln</a> met privately with Edward in the castle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[291]</a></sup> They informed Edward that if he were to resign as monarch, his son Prince Edward would succeed him, but if he failed to do so, his son might be disinherited as well, and the crown given to an alternative candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[292]</a></sup> In tears, Edward agreed to abdicate, and on 21 January, Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Trussell" title="William Trussell">William Trussell</a>, representing the kingdom as a whole, withdrew his homage and formally ended Edward's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[293]</a></sup> A proclamation was sent to London, announcing that Edward, now known just as Edward of Caernarvon, had freely resigned his kingdom and that Prince Edward would succeed him. The coronation took place at Westminster Abbey on 2 February 1327.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[294]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_.281327.29">Death (1327)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Death (1327)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_aftermath">Death and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Death and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of walkway in Berkeley Castle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg/170px-Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg/255px-Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg/340px-Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edward_II%27s_cell_-_geograph.org.uk_-_585477.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Covered-walkway leading to a cell within <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Castle" title="Berkeley Castle">Berkeley Castle</a>, by tradition associated with Edward's imprisonment</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Those opposed to the new government began to make plans to free Edward, and Roger Mortimer decided to move Edward to the more secure location of <a href="/wiki/Berkeley_Castle" title="Berkeley Castle">Berkeley Castle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, where the former King arrived around 5 April 1327.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[295]</a></sup> Once at the castle, Edward was kept in the custody of Mortimer's brother-in-law, Thomas Berkeley, and John Maltravers, who were given £5 a day for Edward's maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P541_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P541-310">[296]</a></sup> It is unclear how well cared for Edward was; the records show luxury goods being bought on his behalf, but some chroniclers suggest that he was often mistreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips2011P541_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips2011P541-310">[296]</a></sup> A poem, the <i><a href="/wiki/Lament_of_Edward_II" title="Lament of Edward II">Lament of Edward II</a></i>, was once thought to have been written by Edward during his imprisonment, although modern scholarship has cast doubt on this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poemdetails_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poemdetails-311">[297]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[nb 15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concerns continued to be raised over fresh plots to liberate Edward, some involving the Dominican order and former household knights, and one such attempt got at least as far as breaking into the prison within the castle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[298]</a></sup> As a result of these threats, Edward was moved around to other locations in secret for a period, before returning to permanent custody at the castle in the late summer of 1327.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[299]</a></sup> The political situation remained unstable, and new plots appear to have been formed to free him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[300]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 23 September Edward III was informed that his father had died at Berkeley Castle during the night of 21 September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[301]</a></sup> Most historians agree that Edward II did die at Berkeley on that date, although there is a minority view, described below, that he died much later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MainstreamList_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-MainstreamList-317">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[nb 16]</a></sup> Edward's death was, as Mark Ormrod notes, "suspiciously timely", as it simplified Mortimer's political problems considerably, and most historians believe that Edward probably was murdered on the orders of the new regime, although it is impossible to be certain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[303]</a></sup> Several of the individuals suspected of involvement in the death, including Sir Thomas Gurney, Maltravers and William Ockley, later fled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[304]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[nb 17]</a></sup> If Edward did die from natural causes, his death may have been hastened by <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a> following his imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[306]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rule of Isabella and Mortimer did not last long after the announcement of Edward's death. They made peace with the Scots in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Northampton" title="Treaty of Northampton">Treaty of Northampton</a>, but this move was highly unpopular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[307]</a></sup> Isabella and Mortimer both amassed, and spent, great wealth, and criticism of them mounted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[308]</a></sup> Relations between Mortimer and the young Edward III became strained and in 1330 the King conducted a <i><a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup d'état</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Castle" title="Nottingham Castle">Nottingham Castle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[309]</a></sup> He arrested Mortimer and then executed him on fourteen charges of treason, including the murder of Edward II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[310]</a></sup> Edward III's government sought to blame Mortimer for all of the recent problems, effectively politically rehabilitating the late King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[311]</a></sup> The King spared Isabella, giving her a generous allowance, and she soon returned to public life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[312]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burial_and_cult">Burial and cult</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Burial and cult">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of Edward's tomb" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="845" data-file-width="1126" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg/220px-Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg/330px-Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg/440px-Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edward_ll_tomb_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1704667.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Edward II's tomb at <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Cathedral" title="Gloucester Cathedral">Gloucester Cathedral</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward's body was <a href="/wiki/Embalming" title="Embalming">embalmed</a> at Berkeley Castle, where it was viewed by local leaders from Bristol and Gloucester.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[313]</a></sup> It was then taken to <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Abbey" title="Gloucester Abbey">Gloucester Abbey</a> on 21 October, and on 20 December Edward was buried by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_altar" title="High altar">high altar</a>, the funeral having probably been delayed to allow Edward III to attend in person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[nb 18]</a></sup> Gloucester was probably chosen because other abbeys had refused or been forbidden to take the King's body, and because it was close to Berkeley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[nb 19]</a></sup> The funeral was a grand affair and cost £351 in total, complete with gilt lions, standards painted with <a href="/wiki/Gold_leaf" title="Gold leaf">gold leaf</a> and oak barriers to manage the anticipated crowds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[318]</a></sup> Edward III's government probably hoped to put a veneer of normality over the recent political events, increasing the legitimacy of the young King's own reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[319]</a></sup></p>
<p>A temporary wooden <a href="/wiki/Effigy" title="Effigy">effigy</a> with a copper crown was made for the funeral; this is the first known use of a funeral effigy in England, and was probably necessary because of the condition of the King's body, which had been dead for three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[320]</a></sup> Edward's heart was removed, placed in a silver container, and later buried with Isabella at <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Greyfriars" title="Christ Church Greyfriars">Newgate Church</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[321]</a></sup> His tomb includes a very early example of an English <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> effigy, with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest_tomb" title="Chest tomb">tomb-chest</a> and a canopy made of <a href="/wiki/Oolite" title="Oolite">oolite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Purbeck_stone" title="Purbeck stone">Purbeck stone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffy2003PP119GloucesterWebsite_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy2003PP119GloucesterWebsite-341">[322]</a></sup> Edward was buried in the shirt, <a href="/wiki/Coif" title="Coif">coif</a> and gloves from his coronation, and his effigy depicts him as king, holding a <a href="/wiki/Sceptre" title="Sceptre">sceptre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Globus_cruciger" title="Globus cruciger">orb</a>, and wearing a strawberry-leaf crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[323]</a></sup> The effigy features a pronounced lower lip, and may be a close likeness of Edward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[nb 20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The King's tomb rapidly became a popular site for visitors, probably encouraged by the local monks, who lacked an existing pilgrimage attraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[326]</a></sup> Visitors donated extensively to the abbey, allowing the monks to rebuild much of the surrounding church in the 1330s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffy2003PP119GloucesterWebsite_341-1"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy2003PP119GloucesterWebsite-341">[322]</a></sup> Miracles reportedly took place at the tomb, and modifications had to be made to enable visitors to walk around it in larger numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[327]</a></sup> The chronicler <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_the_Baker" title="Geoffrey the Baker">Geoffrey de Baker</a> depicted Edward as a saintly, tortured <a href="/wiki/Martyr" title="Martyr">martyr</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II</a> gave royal support for an unsuccessful bid to have Edward <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonised</a> in 1395.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[328]</a></sup> The tomb was opened by officials in 1855, uncovering a wooden coffin, still in good condition, and a sealed lead coffin inside it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[329]</a></sup> The tomb remains in what is now <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_Cathedral" title="Gloucester Cathedral">Gloucester Cathedral</a>, and was extensively restored between 2007 and 2008 at a cost of over £100,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[330]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Controversial_death"></span></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Controversy rapidly surrounded Edward's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[331]</a></sup> With Mortimer's execution in 1330, rumours began to circulate that Edward had been murdered at Berkeley Castle. Accounts that he had been killed by the insertion of a red-hot iron or <a href="/wiki/Fire_iron" title="Fire iron">poker</a> into his <a href="/wiki/Anus" title="Anus">anus</a> slowly began to circulate, possibly as a result of deliberate propaganda; chroniclers in the mid-1330s and 1340s spread this account further, supported in later years by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geoffrey_le_Baker" title="Geoffrey le Baker">Geoffrey le Baker</a>'s colourful account of the killing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[332]</a></sup> It became incorporated into most later histories of Edward, typically being linked to his possible homosexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[333]</a></sup> Most historians now dismiss this account of Edward's death, querying the logic in Edward's captors murdering him in such an easily detectable fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[nb 21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another set of theories surround the possibility that Edward did not really die in 1327. These theories typically involve the "<a href="/wiki/Fieschi_Letter" title="Fieschi Letter">Fieschi Letter</a>", sent to Edward III by an Italian priest called Manuel Fieschi, who claimed that Edward escaped Berkeley Castle in 1327 with the help of a servant, and ultimately retired to become a hermit in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[336]</a></sup> The body buried at Gloucester Cathedral was said to be that of the porter of Berkeley Castle, killed by the assassins and presented by them to Isabella as Edward's corpse to avoid punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[337]</a></sup> The letter is often linked to an account of Edward III meeting with a man called William the Welshman in <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> in 1338, who claimed to be Edward II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[338]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some parts of the letter's content are considered broadly accurate by historians, although other aspects of its account have been criticised as implausible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[339]</a></sup> A few historians have supported versions of its narrative. <a href="/wiki/Paul_C._Doherty" title="Paul C. Doherty">Paul Doherty</a> questions the veracity of the letter and the identity of William the Welshman, but nonetheless has suspicions that Edward may have survived his imprisonment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[340]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Popular_history" title="Popular history">popular historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Alison_Weir" title="Alison Weir">Alison Weir</a> believes the events in the letter to be essentially true, using the letter to argue that Isabella was innocent of murdering Edward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[341]</a></sup> The historian <a href="/wiki/Ian_Mortimer_(historian)" title="Ian Mortimer (historian)">Ian Mortimer</a> suggests that the story in Fieschi's letter is broadly accurate, but argues that it was in fact Mortimer and Isabella who had Edward secretly released, and who then faked his death, a fiction later maintained by Edward III when he came to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[342]</a></sup> Ian Mortimer's account was criticised by most scholars when it was first published, in particular by historian <a href="/wiki/David_Carpenter_(historian)" title="David Carpenter (historian)">David Carpenter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[343]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[nb 22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Edward_as_king">Edward as king</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Edward as king">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingship.2C_government_and_law">Kingship, government and law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Kingship, government and law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:341px;max-width:341px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="345" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg/170px-Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg/255px-Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg/340px-Seal_of_Edward_II.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:165px;max-width:165px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="336" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg/163px-Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg/245px-Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg/326px-Seal_of_Edward_II-2.jpg 2x" width="163"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Edward's <a href="/wiki/Great_Seal_of_the_Realm" title="Great Seal of the Realm">Great Seal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward was ultimately a failure as a king; the historian Michael Prestwich observes that he "was lazy and incompetent, liable to outbursts of temper over unimportant issues, yet indecisive when it came to major issues", echoed by Roy Haines' description of Edward as "incompetent and vicious", and as "no man of business".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[345]</a></sup> Edward did not just delegate routine government to his subordinates, but also higher level decision making, and Pierre Chaplais argues that he "was not so much an incompetent king as a reluctant one", preferring to rule through a powerful deputy, such as Piers Gaveston or Hugh Despenser the Younger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[346]</a></sup> Edward's willingness to promote his favourites had serious political consequences, although he also attempted to buy the loyalty of a wider grouping of nobles through grants of money and fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[347]</a></sup> Edward could take a keen interest in the minutiae of administration, however, and on occasion engaged in the details of a wide range of issues across England and his wider domains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[nb 23]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of Edward's persistent challenges through most of his reign was a shortage of money; of the debts he inherited from his father, around £60,000 was still owing in the 1320s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[350]</a></sup> The King worked his way through many treasurers and other financial officials, few of whom stayed long, raising revenues through often unpopular taxes, and requisitioning goods using his right of prise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestwich2003PP94Phillips2011PP218_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2003PP94Phillips2011PP218-374">[351]</a></sup> He also took out many loans, first through the Frescobaldi family, and then through his banker Antonio Pessagno.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestwich2003PP94Phillips2011PP218_374-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2003PP94Phillips2011PP218-374">[351]</a></sup> Edward took a strong interest in financial matters towards the end of his reign, distrusting his own officials and attempting to increase revenues directly by cutting back on the expenses of his own household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[352]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward was responsible for implementing royal justice through his network of judges and officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[353]</a></sup> It is uncertain to what extent Edward took a personal interest in dispensing justice, but he appears to have involved himself to some degree during the first part of his reign, and to have increasingly intervened in person after 1322.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[354]</a></sup> Edward made extensive use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_law_(Roman)" title="Civil law (Roman)">Roman civil law</a> during his reign when arguing in defence of his causes and favourites, which may have attracted criticism from those who perceived this as abandoning the established principles of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Common_Law" title="English Common Law">English Common Law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[355]</a></sup> Edward was also criticised by contemporaries for allowing the Despensers to exploit the royal justice system for their own ends; the Despensers certainly appear to have abused the system, although just how widely they did so is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[356]</a></sup> Amid the political turbulence, armed gangs and violence spread across England under Edward's reign, destabilising the position of many of the local <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a>; much of Ireland similarly disintegrated into anarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[357]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under Edward's rule, parliament's importance grew as a means of making political decisions and answering petitions, although as the historian Claire Valente notes, the gatherings were "still as much an event as an institution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[358]</a></sup> After 1311, parliament began to include, in addition to the barons, the representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burgess_(title)" title="Burgess (title)">burgesses</a>, who in later years would constitute the "<a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">commons</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[359]</a></sup> Although parliament often opposed raising fresh taxes, active opposition to Edward came largely from the barons, rather than parliament itself, although the barons did seek to use the parliamentary meetings as a way of giving legitimacy to their long-standing political demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[360]</a></sup> After resisting it for many years, Edward began intervening in parliament in the second half of his reign to achieve his own political aims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[361]</a></sup> It remains unclear whether Edward was deposed in 1327 by a formal gathering of parliament or simply a gathering of the political classes alongside an existing parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[362]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Court">Court</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Court">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cambridge_1575_colour_Trinity_College.jpg"><img alt="Detail of 1575 map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="240" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Cambridge_1575_colour_Trinity_College.jpg/170px-Cambridge_1575_colour_Trinity_College.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/Cambridge_1575_colour_Trinity_College.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cambridge_1575_colour_Trinity_College.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1575 map showing the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Hall,_Cambridge" title="King's Hall, Cambridge">King's Hall</a> (top left) founded by Edward</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Edward's royal court was itinerant, travelling around the country with the King.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[363]</a></sup> When housed in Westminster Palace, the court occupied a complex of two halls, seven chambers and three <a href="/wiki/Chapel" title="Chapel">chapels</a>, along with other smaller rooms, but, due to the Scottish conflict, the court spent much of its time in Yorkshire and Northumbria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[364]</a></sup> At the heart of the court was Edward's royal household, in turn divided into the "hall" and the "chamber"; the size of the household varied over time, but in 1317 was around 500 strong, including household knights, squires, kitchen and transport staff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[365]</a></sup> The household was surrounded by a wider group of courtiers, and appears to have also attracted a circle of prostitutes and criminal elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[366]</a></sup></p>
<p>Music and minstrels were very popular at Edward's court, but hunting appears to have been a much less important activity, and there was little emphasis on chivalric events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[367]</a></sup> Edward was interested in buildings and paintings, but less so in literary works, which were not extensively sponsored at court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[368]</a></sup> There was an extensive use of gold and silver plates, jewels and enamelling at court, which would have been richly decorated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestwich2006P69_392-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich2006P69-392">[369]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[nb 24]</a></sup> Edward kept a <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a> as a pet and, as a young man, took a <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a> with him on campaign to Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[370]</a></sup> The court could be entertained in exotic ways: by an Italian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snake-charmer" title="Snake-charmer">snake-charmer</a> in 1312, and the following year by 54 nude French dancers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[371]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-397"><a href="#cite_note-397">[nb 25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Edward's approach to religion was normal for the period, and the historian Michael Prestwich describes him as "a man of wholly conventional religious attitudes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestiwch2006P67_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prestiwch2006P67-398">[373]</a></sup> There were daily chapel services and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alms-giving" title="Alms-giving">alms-giving</a> at his court, and Edward blessed the sick, although he did this less often than his predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestiwch2006P67_398-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prestiwch2006P67-398">[373]</a></sup> Edward remained close to the Dominican Order, who had helped to educate him, and followed their advice in asking for papal permission to be anointed with the Holy Oil of St Thomas of Canterbury in 1319; this request was refused, causing the King some embarrassment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[374]</a></sup> Edward supported the expansion of the universities during his reign, establishing <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Hall,_Cambridge" title="King's Hall, Cambridge">King's Hall</a> in Cambridge to promote training in religious and civil law, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriel_College" title="Oriel College">Oriel College</a> in Oxford and a <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Dublin#History" title="Trinity College, Dublin">short-lived university in Dublin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-400"><a href="#cite_note-400">[375]</a></sup></p>
<p>Edward enjoyed a good relationship with <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_V" title="Pope Clement V">Pope Clement V</a>, despite the King's repeated intervention in the operation of the English Church, including punishing bishops with whom he disagreed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-401"><a href="#cite_note-401">[376]</a></sup> With Clement's support, Edward attempted to gain the financial support of the English Church for his military campaigns in Scotland, including taxation and borrowing money against the funds gathered for the crusades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[377]</a></sup> The English Church did relatively little to influence or moderate Edward's behaviour during his reign, possibly because of the bishops' self-interest and concern for their own protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[378]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a>, elected in 1316, sought Edward's support for a new crusade, and was also inclined to support him politically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_263_404-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_263-404">[379]</a></sup> In 1317, in exchange for papal support in his war with Scotland, Edward agreed to recommence paying the annual Papal tribute, which had been first agreed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_England" title="John of England">King John</a> in 1213; Edward soon ceased the payments, however, and the King never offered his homage, another part of the 1213 agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_2011_263_404-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips_2011_263-404">[379]</a></sup> In 1325 Edward asked Pope John to instruct the Irish Church to openly preach in favour of his right to rule the island, and to threaten to excommunicate any contrary voices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-405"><a href="#cite_note-405">[380]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Edward_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oriel_College_Charter.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of medieval charter" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4213" data-file-width="6627" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Oriel_College_Charter.jpg/220px-Oriel_College_Charter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Oriel_College_Charter.jpg/330px-Oriel_College_Charter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Oriel_College_Charter.jpg/440px-Oriel_College_Charter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oriel_College_Charter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oriel_College" title="Oriel College">Oriel College</a>'s 1326 charter from Edward</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>No chronicler for this period is entirely trustworthy or unbiased, often because their accounts were written to support a particular cause, but it is clear that most contemporary chroniclers were highly critical of Edward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-406"><a href="#cite_note-406">[381]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychronicon" title="Polychronicon">Polychronicon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vita_Edwardi_Secundi" title="Vita Edwardi Secundi">Vita Edwardi Secundi</a></i>, <i>Vita et Mors Edwardi Secundi</i> and the <i>Gesta Edwardi de Carnarvon</i> for example all condemned the King's personality, habits and choice of companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-407"><a href="#cite_note-407">[382]</a></sup> Other records from his reign show criticism of Edward by his contemporaries, including the Church and members of his own household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-408"><a href="#cite_note-408">[383]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_song" title="Political song">Political songs</a> were written about him, complaining about his failure in war and his oppressive government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-409"><a href="#cite_note-409">[384]</a></sup> Later in the 14th century, some chroniclers, such as Geoffrey le Baker and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Ringstead" title="Thomas Ringstead">Thomas Ringstead</a>, rehabilitated Edward, presenting him as a martyr and a potential saint, although this tradition died out in later years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-410"><a href="#cite_note-410">[385]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians in the 16th and 17th centuries focused on Edward's relationship with Gaveston, drawing comparisons between Edward's reign and the events surrounding the relationship of the <a href="/wiki/Jean_Louis_de_Nogaret_de_La_Valette" title="Jean Louis de Nogaret de La Valette">Duc d'Épernon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry III of France</a>, and between the <a href="/wiki/George_Villiers,_1st_Duke_of_Buckingham" title="George Villiers, 1st Duke of Buckingham">Duke of Buckingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-411"><a href="#cite_note-411">[386]</a></sup> In the first half of the 19th century, popular historians such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Knight_(publisher)" title="Charles Knight (publisher)">Charles Knight</a> popularised Edward's life with the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian public</a>, focusing on the King's relationship with his favourites and, increasingly, alluding to his possible homosexuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-412"><a href="#cite_note-412">[387]</a></sup> From the 1870s onwards, however, open academic discussion of Edward's sexuality was circumscribed by changing English values. By the start of the 20th century, English schools were being advised by the government to avoid overt discussion of Edward's personal relationships in history lessons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-413"><a href="#cite_note-413">[388]</a></sup> Views on Edward's sexuality have continued to develop over the years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestwich_2003_72_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich_2003_72-41">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of the 19th century, more administrative records from the period had become available to historians such as <a href="/wiki/William_Stubbs" title="William Stubbs">William Stubbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Frederick_Tout" title="Thomas Frederick Tout">Thomas Tout</a> and J. C. Davies, who focused on the development of the English constitutional and governmental system during his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaughPhillipsP241_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaughPhillipsP241-414">[389]</a></sup> Although critical of what they regarded as Edward's inadequacies as a king, they also emphasised the growth of the role of parliament and the reduction in personal royal authority under Edward II, which they perceived as positive developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-415"><a href="#cite_note-415">[390]</a></sup> During the 1970s the historiography of Edward's reign shifted away from this model, supported by the further publishing of records from the period in the last quarter of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaughPhillipsP241_414-1"><a href="#cite_note-WaughPhillipsP241-414">[389]</a></sup> The work of Jeffrey Denton, Jeffrey Hamilton, <a href="/wiki/John_Maddicott" title="John Maddicott">John Maddicott</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._R._S._Phillips" title="J. R. S.
